<p>Historian George Herring argues that Adams was the most independent-minded of the founders.<sup id="cite_ref-George_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Though he aligned with the Federalists, he was somewhat a party unto himself, disagreeing with the Federalists as much as he did the Democratic-Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> He was often described as "prickly", but his tenacity was fed by good decisions made in the face of universal opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-George_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams was often combative, which diminished presidential decorum, as Adams himself admitted in his old age: "[As president] I refused to suffer in silence. I sighed, sobbed, and groaned, and sometimes screeched and screamed. And I must confess to my shame and sorrow that I sometimes swore."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams' resolve to advance peace with France, rather than to continue hostilities, especially reduced his popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> This played an important role in his reelection defeat, however he was so pleased with the outcome that he had it engraved on his tombstone. Adams spent much of his term at home in Massachusetts, ignoring the details of political patronage nursed by other office holders.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-War_and_peace_with_France">Quasi-War and peace with France</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fries_Rebellion" title="Fries Rebellion" class="mw-redirect">Fries Rebellion</a></div>
<p>The president's term was marked by disputes concerning the country's role, if any, in the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">expanding conflict</a> in Europe, where <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">Britain and France were at war</a>. Hamilton and the Federalists supported Britain, while Jefferson and the Democratic-Republicans favored France.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The French had supported Jefferson for president and became even more belligerent at his loss.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> When Adams entered office, he decided to continue Washington's policy of staying out of the European war. The intense battle over the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> in 1795 had previously polarized politics throughout the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The French saw America as Britain's junior partner and began seizing American merchant ships that were trading with the British. Nevertheless, most Americans were initially pro-French due to France's assistance during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a>, and would not have sufficiently rallied behind anyone to stop France.<sup id="cite_ref-k13_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k13-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/220px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/330px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/440px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="816" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon depicts the XYZ Affair – America is a female being plundered by Frenchmen. (1798)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sentiments changed with the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a>, in which the French demanded huge bribes before any discussions could begin regarding American complaints; this substantially weakened popular American support of France. The pro-French Jeffersonians lost support and quickly became the minority as many began to demand full-scale war. The affair heightened fears of sedition by the administration's opponents and legislation was introduced in response. The president knew that America would be unable to win a conflict, as France at the time was dominating the fight in most of Europe. Adams therefore pursued a strategy whereby American ships harassed French ships in an effort sufficient to stem the French assaults on American interests. This was the undeclared naval war between the U.S. and France–the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> which broke out in 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There was danger of invasion from the more powerful French forces, so Adams and the Federalist congress built up the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army" class="mw-redirect">army</a>, bringing back Washington as its commander. Washington wanted Hamilton to be his second-in-command and Adams reluctantly accommodated.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> It became apparent that Hamilton was truly in charge due to Washington's advanced years. The angered president remarked at the time, "Hamilton I know to be a proud Spirited, conceited, aspiring Mortal always pretending to Morality," he wrote, but "with as debauched Morals as old Franklin who is more his Model than anyone I know."<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams also rebuilt the Navy, adding <a href="/wiki/Six_original_United_States_frigates" title="Six original United States frigates" class="mw-redirect">six fast, powerful frigates</a>, most notably the USS <i>Constitution</i>. To pay for the military buildup, Congress imposed new taxes on property: the Direct Tax of 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-k13_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k13-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> It was the first (and last) such federal tax. Taxpayers were angered, especially in southeast Pennsylvania, where the bloodless <a href="/wiki/Fries%27s_Rebellion" title="Fries's Rebellion">Fries's Rebellion</a> broke out among rural German-speaking farmers who protested what they saw as a threat to their republican liberties and to their churches.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton assumed control in the War department, and the rift between Adams' and Hamilton's supporters widened. Many sought to vest Hamilton with command authority over the army, and they also resisted giving prominent Democratic-Republicans positions in the army, which Adams wanted to do in order to gain bipartisan support. By building a large <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, Hamilton's supporters raised popular alarms and played into the hands of the Democratic-Republicans. They also alienated Adams and his large personal following. They shortsightedly viewed the Federalist party as their own tool and ignored the need to pull together the entire nation in the face of war with France. Overall, however, patriotic sentiments and a series of naval victories, popularized the war as well as the president.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1799, Adams surprised many by sending diplomat <a href="/wiki/William_Vans_Murray" title="William Vans Murray">William Vans Murray</a> on a peace mission to France. <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, realizing that the conflict was pointless, signaled his readiness for friendly relations. At the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_1800" title="Convention of 1800">Convention of 1800</a> the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778)" title="Treaty of Alliance (1778)">Treaty of Alliance of 1778</a> was superseded and the United States was then free of foreign entanglements, as Washington had advised in his farewell address. Adams brought in <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> as Secretary of State and demobilized the emergency army.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams proudly avoided war, but deeply split his party in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alien_and_Sedition_Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -67px; left: -52px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,John-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Adams as President" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="376" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8488" data-file-height="9818" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,John-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ADAMS,John-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ADAMS,John-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Adams as President</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the discredit of the XYZ Affair, the Democratic-Republicans' opposition persisted. In the midst of war, which included the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_terror" title="Reign of terror" class="mw-redirect">reign of terror</a> during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, political tensions were incendiary. Some pro-French Democratic-Republicans even fostered a movement in America, similar to the French Revolution, to overthrow the Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> When Democratic-Republicans in some states refused to enforce federal laws, some Federalists voiced the intention to send in an army and force them to capitulate. As the hostility sweeping Europe bled over into America, calls for secession began to reach new heights.<sup id="cite_ref-KnottAlexander_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KnottAlexander-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Some Federalists accused the French and their associated immigrants of provoking civil unrest. In an attempt to quell the uprising, the Federalists introduced, and the Congress passed, a series of laws collectively referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>, which were signed by Adams in 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Congress specifically passed four measures – the <a href="/wiki/Naturalization_Act_of_1798" title="Naturalization Act of 1798">Naturalization Act</a>, the Alien Friends Act, the Alien Enemies Act and the Sedition Act. These statutes were designed to mitigate the threat of secessionists by disallowing their most extreme firebrands. The Naturalization Act increased to 14 years the period of residence required for an immigrant to attain American citizenship (naturalized citizens tended to vote for the Democratic-Republicans.) The Alien Friends Act and the Alien Enemies Act allowed the president to deport any foreigner (from friendly and hostile nations, respectively) which he considered dangerous to the country. The Sedition Act made it a crime to publish "false, scandalous, and malicious writing" against the government or its officials. Punishments included 2–5 years in prison and fines of up to $5,000. Although Adams had not promoted any of these acts, he signed them into law.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The acts became controversial from prosecution thereunder of a Congressman and a number of newspaper editors. Indeed, the Federalist administration initiated fourteen or more indictments under the Sedition Act, as well as suits against five of the six most prominent Democratic-Republican newspapers. The majority of the legal actions began in 1798 and 1799, and went to trial on the eve of the 1800 presidential election–timing that hardly appeared coincidental, according to biographer Ferling. Other historians have cited evidence that the Alien and Sedition Acts were rarely enforced, namely: 1) only 10 convictions under the Sedition Act have been identified; 2) Adams never signed a deportation order; and 3) the sources of expressed furor over the acts were Democratic-Republicans. However, other historians have emphasized that the Acts were employed for political targeting from the outset, causing many aliens to leave the country. The Acts as well allowed for prosecution of many who opposed the Federalists, even on the floor of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> In any case, the election of 1800 in fact became a bitter and volatile contest, with each side expressing extraordinary fear of the other and its policies; after Democratic-Republicans prevailed in the elections of 1800, they used the acts against Federalists before the laws finally expired.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1800">Election of 1800</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="542" data-file-height="446" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1800 Electoral College Vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The death of Washington in 1799 weakened the Federalists, as they lost the one man who united the party. In the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_election_of_1800" title="Presidential election of 1800" class="mw-redirect">presidential election of 1800</a>, Adams and his fellow Federalist candidate, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a>, opposed the Republican ticket of Jefferson and Burr. Hamilton tried his hardest to sabotage Adams' campaign in the hope of boosting Pinckney's chances of winning the presidency. In the end, Adams lost narrowly to Jefferson by 65 to 73 electoral votes, with New York providing the decisive margin.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams' defeat resulted from 1) the stronger organization of the Democratic-Republicans, 2) Federalist disunity, 3) the controversy of the Alien and Sedition Acts, 4) the popularity of Jefferson in the south and 5) the effective politicking of Aaron Burr in <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York State</a>, where the legislature shifted from Federalist to Democratic-Republican on the basis of a few wards in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> controlled by Burr's machine.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the closing months of his term Adams became the first president to occupy the new, but unfinished <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">President's Mansion</a> (later known as the White House) beginning November 1, 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-tavern_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tavern-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> "I pray Heaven to bestow the best of Blessings on this House and all that shall hereafter inhabit it," Adams wrote on his second night in the mansion. "May none but honest and wise Men ever rule under this roof."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After his defeat in the hotly contested election, Adams was depressed when he left office. His son Charles had also recently died from alcoholism, and he was anxious to rejoin his wife Abigail, who had left for Massachusetts months before the inauguration. As a result, he did not attend Jefferson's inauguration, departing the White House at 4:00 a.m. that day, and making him one of only four presidents surviving in office not to attend his successor's inauguration. Adams' correspondence with Jefferson at the time is not indicative of the animosity and resentment that scholars have attributed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_court">Supreme court</span></h4>
<table class="infobox" cellspacing="5" style="width: auto; text-align:left; line-height: 1.2em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 1em; float: left; clear: left;">
<tr>
<th colspan="3" style="line-height: 1.5em; font-size: 110%; background:#DCDCDC; text-align: center;">Supreme Court Appointments by President Adams</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Position</th>
<th scope="col">Name</th>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a></th>
<td>1800 (declined)</td>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a></th>

<td>1799–1829</td>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Moore" title="Alfred Moore">Alfred Moore</a></th>
<td>1800–1804</td>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman,_1832.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/220px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/330px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/440px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1059" data-file-height="1312" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman,_1832.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chief Justice John Marshall (1832)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams named John Marshall as the fourth <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a> to succeed <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Ellsworth" title="Oliver Ellsworth">Oliver Ellsworth</a>, who had retired due to ill health. Marshall's long tenure represents the most lasting influence of the Federalists, as he infused the Constitution with a judicious and carefully reasoned nationalistic interpretation and established the Judicial Branch as the equal of the Executive and Legislative branches.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_judicial_appointments">Other judicial appointments</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_John_Adams" title="List of federal judges appointed by John Adams">List of federal judges appointed by John Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges" title="Midnight Judges" class="mw-redirect">Midnight Judges</a></div>
<p>The lame-duck session of Congress in late 1800 enacted the Judiciary Act of 1801, which created a set of federal appeals courts between the district courts and the Supreme Court. The purpose of the statute was twofold—first, to remedy the defects in the federal judicial system inherent in the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Act_of_1789" title="Judiciary Act of 1789">Judiciary Act of 1789</a>, and second, to enable the defeated Federalists to staff the new judicial offices with loyal Federalists in the face of the party's defeat in 1800–the party had lost control of both houses of congress in addition to the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams filled the vacancies created in this statute by appointing a series of judges, whom his opponents called the "<a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges" title="Midnight Judges" class="mw-redirect">Midnight Judges</a>" because most of them were appointed just days before his presidential term expired. Most of these judges lost their posts when the Jeffersonian Republicans enacted the Judiciary Act of 1802, abolishing the courts created by the Judiciary Act of 1801 and returning the federal courts to their original structure as specified in the 1789 statute.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An unsmiling elderly man sits in a red chair, slightly pointing left." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/220px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/330px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/440px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1266" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Adams, ca 1816, by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_F.B._Morse" title="Samuel F.B. Morse" class="mw-redirect">Samuel F.B. Morse</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum_of_Art" title="Brooklyn Museum of Art" class="mw-redirect">Brooklyn Museum</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams resumed farming at his home <a href="/wiki/Peacefield" title="Peacefield">Peacefield</a> in the town of Quincy; he also began work on an autobiography (which he never finished) and resumed correspondence with such old friends as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Waterhouse" title="Benjamin Waterhouse">Benjamin Waterhouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After Jefferson's retirement from public life in 1809, Adams became more vocal. He published a three-year marathon of letters in the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Patriot_(newspaper)" title="Boston Patriot (newspaper)">Boston Patriot</a></i> newspaper, refuting line-by-line an 1800 pamphlet by Hamilton which attacked his conduct and character. Though Hamilton had died in 1804 in <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">a duel with Aaron Burr</a>, Adams felt the need to vindicate his character against the New Yorker's vehement charges.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The years of retirement in the Adams' household were not without some temporary financial adversity; in 1803 the bank holding his cash reserves of about $13,000 collapsed. Son John Quincy came to the rescue by purchasing from him his properties in Weymouth and Quincy, including Peacefield, for the sum of $12,800.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Daughter <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams_Smith" title="Abigail Adams Smith">Abigail ("Nabby")</a> was married to <a href="/wiki/Congressman" title="Congressman" class="mw-redirect">Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Stephens_Smith" title="William Stephens Smith">William Stephens Smith</a>, but she returned to her parents' home after the failure of the marriage; she died of breast cancer in 1813. His wife Abigail died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid" class="mw-redirect">typhoid</a> on October 28, 1818. His son Thomas and wife Ann, along with seven children, lived with Adams to the end of Adams' life, as well as Louisa Smith (Abigail's niece by her brother William).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Sixteen months before John Adams' death, his son, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, became the sixth president of the United States in 1825, the only son to succeed his father as President until <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Correspondence_with_Jefferson">Correspondence with Jefferson</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An elderly man sits in a red chair with his arms crossed, looking slightly left." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/220px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/330px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/440px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="853" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Adams was nearly 89 when, at the request of his son, John Quincy Adams, he posed a final time for <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a> (1823).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In early 1812, Adams reconciled with Jefferson. Their mutual friend <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>, a fellow signer of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> who had been corresponding with both, encouraged them to reach out to the other. On New Year's Day Adams sent a brief, friendly note to Jefferson to accompany the delivery of "two pieces of homespun," a two-volume collection of lectures on rhetoric by John Quincy Adams. Jefferson replied immediately with a cordial letter, and the two men revived their friendship, which they sustained by mail. The correspondence that they resumed in 1812 lasted the rest of their lives, and has been hailed as among their great legacies of American literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Their letters represent an insight into both the period and the minds of the two revolutionary leaders and Presidents. The missives lasted fourteen years, and consisted of 158 letters–109 from Adams and 49 from Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_99-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The two men discussed "natural aristocracy." Jefferson said, "The natural aristocracy I consider as the most precious gift of nature for the instruction, the trusts, and government of society. And indeed it would have been inconsistent in creation to have formed man for the social state, and not to have provided virtue and wisdom enough to manage the concerns of society. May we not even say that the form of government is best which provides most effectually for a pure selection of these natural [aristocrats] into the offices of government?"<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams wondered if it ever would be so clear who these people were, "Your distinction between natural and artificial aristocracy does not appear to me well founded. Birth and wealth are conferred on some men as imperiously by nature, as genius, strength, or beauty. . . . When aristocracies are established by human laws and honour, wealth, and power are made hereditary by municipal laws and political institutions, then I acknowledge artificial aristocracy to commence." It would always be true, Adams argued, that fate would bestow influence on some men for reasons other than true wisdom and virtue. That being the way of nature, he thought such "talents" were natural. A good government, therefore, had to account for that reality.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="image"><img alt="3 marble sarcophagi, one in the foreground, 2 in the background are seen. 2 are seen with flags of the United States at the top." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/220px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/330px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/440px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tombs of John Adams (far) and John Quincy Adams (near), in family crypt at <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church_(Quincy,_Massachusetts)" title="United First Parish Church (Quincy, Massachusetts)" class="mw-redirect">United First Parish Church</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Less than a month before his death, Adams issued a statement about the destiny of the United States, which historians such as <a href="/wiki/Joy_Hakim" title="Joy Hakim">Joy Hakim</a> have characterized as a "warning" for his fellow citizens: "My best wishes, in the joys, and festivities, and the solemn services of that day on which will be completed the fiftieth year from its birth, of the independence of the United States: a memorable epoch in the annals of the human race, destined in future history to form the brightest or the blackest page, according to the use or the abuse of those political institutions by which they shall, in time to come, be shaped by the human mind."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On July 4, 1826, the fiftieth anniversary of the adoption of the Declaration of Independence, Adams died at his home in Quincy, at approximately 6:20 PM.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Jefferson died earlier the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams' crypt lies at <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church_(Quincy,_Massachusetts)" title="United First Parish Church (Quincy, Massachusetts)" class="mw-redirect">United First Parish Church</a> in Quincy, Massachusetts, with his wife Abigail and son John Quincy Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_philosophy_and_views">Political philosophy and views</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h3>
<p>Adams never bought a slave and declined on principle to utilize slave labor, saying, "I have, through my whole life, held the practice of slavery in such abhorrence, that I have never owned a negro or any other slave, though I have lived for many years in times, when the practice was not disgraceful, when the best men in my vicinity thought it not inconsistent with their character, and when it has cost me thousands of dollars for the labor and subsistence of free men, which I might have saved by the purchase of negroes at times when they were very cheap."<sup id="cite_ref-RJEvans_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RJEvans-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams generally tried to keep the issue out of national politics, because of the anticipated southern response during a time when unity was needed to achieve independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He spoke out in 1777 against a bill to emancipate slaves in Massachusetts, saying that the issue was presently too divisive, and so the legislation should "sleep for a time."<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He also was against use of black soldiers in the Revolution, due to opposition from southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Slavery was abolished in Massachusetts about 1780, when it was forbidden by implication in the Declaration of Rights that John Adams wrote into the Massachusetts Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Abigail Adams, on the other hand, vocally opposed slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._1_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._1-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_monarchism">Accusations of monarchism</span></h3>
<p>Throughout his lifetime Adams expressed controversial and shifting views regarding the virtues of monarchical and hereditary political institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Mark_O._Hatfield.2C_Vice_Pres_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_O._Hatfield.2C_Vice_Pres-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> At times he conveyed substantial support for these approaches,<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Family_Letters_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Family_Letters-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> suggesting for example that "hereditary monarchy or aristocracy" are the "only institutions that can possibly preserve the laws and liberties of the people."<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Family_Letters_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Family_Letters-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Yet at other times he distanced himself from such ideas, calling himself "a mortal and irreconcilable enemy to Monarchy" and "no friend to hereditary limited monarchy in America."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Such denials did not assuage his critics, and Adams was often accused of being a Monarchist.<sup id="cite_ref-John_.26_Abigail_Adams_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_.26_Abigail_Adams-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many of these attacks are considered to have been scurrilous, including suggestions that he was planning to "crown himself king" and "grooming John Quincy as heir to the throne".<sup id="cite_ref-John_.26_Abigail_Adams_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_.26_Abigail_Adams-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Peter Shaw has argued that: "[T]he inevitable attacks on Adams, crude as they were, stumbled on a truth that he did not admit to himself. He was leaning toward monarchy and aristocracy (as distinct from kings and aristocrats) at the time he wrote 'Davila', though he did not directly reveal this in its essays. Decidedly, sometime after he became vice-president, Adams concluded that the United States would have to adopt a hereditary legislature and a monarch... and he outlined a plan by which state conventions would appoint hereditary senators while a national one appointed a president for life."<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw.2C_p230-237_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw.2C_p230-237-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> In contradiction to such notions, Adams asserted in a letter to Thomas Jefferson: "If you suppose that I have ever had a design or desire of attempting to introduce a government of King, Lords and Commons, or in other words an hereditary Executive, or an hereditary Senate, either into the government of the United States, or that of any individual state, in this country, you are wholly mistaken. There is not such a thought expressed or intimated in any public writing or private letter of mine, and I may safely challenge all of mankind to produce such a passage and quote the chapter and verse."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span></h2>
<p>Adams was raised a <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a>, since his ancestors were Puritans. According to biographer McCullough, "as his family and friends knew, Adams was both a devout Christian, and an independent thinker".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> In a letter to Benjamin Rush, Adams credited religion with the success of his ancestors since their migration to the New World in the 1630s.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams was educated at Harvard when the influence of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a> was growing there, and sometimes used deistic terms in his speeches and writing.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> He also believed that regular church service was beneficial to man's moral sense. Everett (1966) concludes that "Adams strove for a religion based on a common sense sort of reasonableness" and maintained that religion must change and evolve toward perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Fielding (1940) argues that Adams' beliefs synthesized Puritan, deist, and <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a> concepts. Adams at one point said that Christianity had originally been <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelatory</a>, but was being misinterpreted and misused in the service of superstition, fraud, and unscrupulous power.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Goff (1993) acknowledges Fielding's "persuasive argument that Adams never was a deist because he allowed the suspension of the laws of nature and believed that evil was internal, not the result of external institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Frazer (2004) notes that while Adams shared many perspectives with deists, "Adams clearly was not a deist. Deism rejected any and all supernatural activity and intervention by God; consequently, deists did not believe in miracles or God's providence....Adams, however, did believe in miracles, providence, and, to a certain extent, the Bible as revelation."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Frazer further argues that Adams' "theistic rationalism, like that of the other Founders, was a sort of middle ground between Protestantism and deism."<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> By contrast, David L. Holmes has argued that Adams, beginning as a Congregationalist, ended his days as a Christian Unitarian, accepting central tenets of the Unitarian creed, but also accepting Jesus as the redeemer of humanity and the biblical account of his miracles as true.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Like many of his Protestant contemporaries, Adams criticized the claims to universal authority made by the Roman Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1796, Adams denounced political opponent <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>'s deistic criticisms of Christianity in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i>, saying, "The Christian religion is, above all the religions that ever prevailed or existed in ancient or modern times, the religion of wisdom, virtue, equity and humanity, let the Blackguard Paine say what he will."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biographies">Biographies</span></h2>
<p>Adams' grandson <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams,_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams, Sr.">Charles Francis Adams, Sr.</a> edited the first two volumes of <i>The Works of John Adams, Esq., Second President of the United States</i>. This was published between 1850 and 1856 by Charles C. Little and James Brown in Boston. The first seven chapters were produced by Adams' son John Quincy Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The premier modern biography was <i>Honest John Adams</i>, a 1933 biography by the noted French specialist in American history Gilbert Chinard, who came to Adams after writing his acclaimed 1929 biography of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. For a generation, Chinard's work was regarded as the best life of Adams, and it is still a key factor in determining the themes of Adams' biographical and historical scholarship. Following the opening of the Adams family papers in the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Page_Smith" title="Page Smith">Page Smith</a> published the first major biography to use these previously inaccessible primary sources; his biography won a 1962 <a href="/wiki/Bancroft_Prize" title="Bancroft Prize">Bancroft Prize</a> but was criticized for its scanting of Adams' intellectual life and its diffuseness. In 1975, Peter Shaw published <i>The Character of John Adams,</i> a thematic biography noted for its psychological insight into Adams' life. The 1992 character study by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_J._Ellis" title="Joseph J. Ellis" class="mw-redirect">Joseph J. Ellis</a>, <i>Passionate Sage: The Character and Legacy of John Adams,</i> was Ellis's first major publishing success and remains one of the most useful and insightful studies of Adams' personality. In 1992, the Revolutionary War historian and biographer <a href="/wiki/John_E._Ferling" title="John E. Ferling">John E. Ferling</a> published his acclaimed <i>John Adams,</i> also noted for its psychological sensitivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, historian David McCullough published a biography entitled <i><a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(book)" title="John Adams (book)">John Adams</a>,</i> that won various awards. McCullough's biography was the basis for a 2008 <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(TV_miniseries)" title="John Adams (TV miniseries)" class="mw-redirect">TV miniseries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="border-top: 5px solid #bebebe;"><small><b>Notes and references</b></small></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="text-align:left;"><small>1. While Adams won the Vice Presidency in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1789" title="United States presidential election, 1789" class="mw-redirect">1789</a> as well, he was not the candidate of the Federalist Party, which had not yet formed.</small><br />
<small>2. Technically, Adams was a presidential candidate in 1792 and Pinckney was a presidential candidate in 1796. Prior to the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a> in 1804, each presidential elector could cast two ballots; the highest vote-getter would become President and the runner-up would become Vice President. Thus, in 1792, with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> as the prohibitive favorite for President, the Federalist party fielded Adams as a presidential candidate, with the intention that he be elected to the Vice Presidency. Similarly, in 1796 and 1800, the Federalist party fielded two candidates, Adams and Thomas Pinckney in 1796 and Adams and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Pinckney</a> in 1800, with the intention that Adams be elected President and Pinckney be elected Vice President.</small></td>
</tr>
